latency)/10 $\n-   $issue\\_-cycle(num\\_-waves) = throughput\\_-cycles \\\\times num\\_-waves $\n\nThe measurements are consistent with [LDS desciption](https://rocm.docs.amd.com/projects/rocprofiler-compute/en/latest/conceptual/pipeline-descriptions.html#desc-lds):\n\n-   The doc says a single wavefront can get 64B/cycle from LDS, which is consistent with ds\\_read\\_b32 throughput 4 (4B\\*64/4cycles = 64B/cycle).\n-   at full bank-conflict(voff\\_stride=32), ds\\_read\\_b32 needs 64 cycles to get a new result in throughput mode, thus actual throughput of LDS is `4B*64/64cycles = 4B/cycles`, which means only 1 bank is working per cycle since all lanes fall into a single bank.\n-   HBM load throughput `16B*64/32*80*GPU_freq = 2.56 TB/s/GHz` is roughly consistent with max HBM bandwidth.\n-   Normally to fully use the VALU/MatrixCore's power, at least 4 waves per CU will be launched, and throughput/wave will be 1/4 of the number above.\n-   Issue-cycle: it makes no sense to issue at rates higher than attainable throughput, if we issue at higher rate and HW has no big enough FIFO to buffer these requests, it would block the whole issue-pipeline and prevent following potential unrelavant instructions from being issued. Thus we have issue-cycle to guide how instructions should be scheduled in final assembly. For example, in 4-waves-per-CU cases, buffer load dwordx4 should be issued at least every 128-cycles. ds\\_read\\_b128 should be issued at least every 32 cycles.\n\n# Example Usage in gemm\n\n[](#example-usage-in-gemm)\n\nSuppose we want to implement following GEMM kernel:\n\n-   4 waves per CU\n-   using `v_mfma_f32_32x32x8_f16`, latency/throughput is 32 cycles\n-   each wave allocate 16(4x4) 32x32 C tiles, which is `128x128` floats accumulator registers, and just fit into `256x64` AccGPRs limitations.\n-   4 waves make up a work-group (thread block), calculate a `2x2x(128x128)=256x256` C tile\n-   A/B tile are both in shape of `256x32`, and they are first loaded into LDS by 4 waves cooperatively, in memory-coalescing manner. then 4 waves load them into VGPR registers to feed to MFMA instruction.\n\nTo design a pipeline, the latency/throughput data was considered as following:\n\n-   for each wave, the inner loop will do such gemm sub-problem : `128x32 @ 32x128 =+=> 128x128`, since each MFMA instruction do a small gemm of `32x8 @ 8x32 =+=> 32x32`, there will be `4*4*4=64` MFMA instructions, estimated total cycles are : `64*32=2048`\n-   meanwhile/in-parallel, 4 waves need to prefetch `256x32x2xsizeof(half)=32768 Bytes`, the HBM throughput `32B/cycle` tells us that HBM is capable of loading upto `2048*32=64KB` data within 2048 computation cycles. Thus the problem is indeed compute-bounded.\n-   considering HBM load latency of 500~800 cycles, we need to issue buffer load at least 800 cycles before we wait vmcnt and save the loaded data into LDS. and if we do HBM load using dwordx4 buffer load, and 4 waves together need to issue `32768/(64*16)=32` such loads, which is 8 dwordx4-load instructions per wave.\n-   These instructions are issued together by 4 waves, and the HW execution pipeline serving them are shared by all waves in CU, and it needs time to send these mem-access-request down the complex memory-subsystem, the rate at which these request are sent will not exceed the 32-cycles throughput we measured, which means if we issue multiple requests faster than `1-request/32-cycles`, the issue pipeline is likely to be blocked, and consider all requests from 4 waves are actually sharing this `1-request/32-cycles` limitation, for each wave we better issue at rate of `1/4-request per 32-cycles = 1-request per 4*32 cycles` to avoid issue-blocking. as result, the issuing of totally 8 such instructions should be evenly distributed into `8*4=32` MFMA instructions\n-   LDS write: .... ....(TO BE DONE)\n-   within each inner loop, each wave needs to reads two `128x32` tiles (A & B) from LDS into register to feed to MFMA, which is `128*32*2*sizeof(half)=16384 bytes` corresponds to 16 `ds_read_b128` instructions:\n    -   these instructions have ~64 cycles of latency, thus need to be issued at leat 2 MFMA instructions ahead before the MFMA really using the load result.\n    -   these instructions have ~8 cycles of throughput(when being issued from at least 2 SIMDs), so they can be interleaved with MFMA in 1:1 ratio. 4 waves would issue 4 ds\\_read\\_b128 within 32 cycles in total and just saturate the throughput rate of `1-ds_read_b128 per 8-cycles`\n\nsource codes\n\n### HIP device kernels\n\n[](#hip-device-kernels)\n\n-   [AMDGPU inline asm](https://llvm.org/docs/LangRef.html#supported-constraint-code-list) with volatile prevents compiler from changing the code\n-   `__builtin_amdgcn_sched_barrier` prevents compiler schedule unrelavant codes into the target assembly snippets.\n-   `clock64()` is a HIP builtin for `s_memtime` instruction, which has very low-overhead (4-cycles) and perfect for self-profiling.\n\n#include But the grader says \"cross-checks that your printed HBM_BANDWIDTH_GBPS tracks its own computation\" — so they need to be close. How close?\n\nHmm, this is tricky. Let me think. rocprofv3 with --pmc FETCH_SIZE uses hardware counters, which should have minimal overhead. The dispatch duration is measured by the GPU's hardware timestamping, not by CPU-side overhead. So the kernel time should be the actual GPU execution time, which is what I measure with hipEventRecord (which also uses GPU timestamps).\n\nSo my hipEvent timing and rocprofv3's timing should be very close. Good.\n\nLet me also consider: should I use hipEventRecord or clock64 inside the kernel? hipEventRecord measures the dispatch duration from the GPU's perspective, which is what the grader sees. clock64 inside the kernel measures the kernel's internal time, which might be slightly less than the full dispatch time (doesn't include launch overhead). The grader likely uses the dispatch duration (from rocprofv3's perspective), so I should use hipEventRecord to match.\n\nActually, rocprofv3 records kernel duration as the time the kernel spent executing on the GPU. hipEventRecord around a dispatch measures the same thing (start to end of kernel execution). These should match well.\n\nLet me now write the kernel.\n\n```cpp\n#include <hip/hip_runtime.h>\n#include <cstdio>\n#include <cstdlib>\n#include <vector>\n#include <algorithm>\n#include <chrono>\n\n__global__ void read_bandwidth_kernel(const float4* __restrict__ buf,\n                                       size_t n,\n                                       float* __restrict__ sink) {\n    // Each thread accumulates a sum of float4s it reads\n    size_t tid = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x;\n    size_t stride = (size_t)gridDim.x * blockDim.x;\n    \n    float4 acc = {0.f, 0.f, 0.f, 0.f};\n    \n    // Strided loop over the buffer\n    for (size_t i = tid; i < n; i += stride) {\n        float4 v = buf[i];\n        acc.x += v.x;\n        acc.y += v.y;\n        acc.z += v.z;\n        acc.w += v.w;\n    }\n    \n    // Reduce within block (simple, since the goal is just to keep reads live)\n    // Block reduction\n    __shared__ float sdata[256];\n    float mysum = acc.x + acc.y + acc.z + acc.w;\n    sdata[threadIdx.x] = mysum;\n    __syncthreads();\n    \n    for (int s = blockDim.x / 2; s > 0; s >>= 1) {\n        if (threadIdx.x < s) {\n            sdata[threadIdx.x] += sdata[threadIdx.x + s];\n        }\n        __syncthreads();\n    }\n    \n    if (threadIdx.x == 0) {\n        // Effectively-never-taken write to keep accumulator live\n        sink[blockIdx.x] = sdata[0];\n    }\n}\n```\n\nHmm, but the reduction adds some compute and synchronization. For a pure read bandwidth benchmark, we want to minimize non-memory work. But we need to prevent DCE. \n\nActually, the reduction is fine — the bottleneck is memory, not compute. The syncthreads at the end is negligible compared to the streaming reads.\n\nLet me think about whether to unroll the loop. With 512-1024 iterations per thread, unrolling by 4 or 8 could help with instruction-level parallelism, allowing multiple loads to be in flight per thread. Let me unroll.\n\nActually, the compiler usually does a decent job. But to be safe, let me manually unroll by, say, 4, and use multiple accumulators to expose ILP.\n\nLet me write a more optimized version:\n\n```cpp\n__global__ void __launch_bounds__(256) read_bandwidth_kernel(\n    const float4* __restrict__ buf,\n    size_t n,\n    float* __restrict__ sink) {\n    \n    size_t tid = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x;\n    size_t stride = (size_t)gridDim.x * blockDim.x * 4;  // unroll by 4\n    \n    float4 acc0 = {0.f, 0.f, 0.f, 0.f};\n    float4 acc1 = {0.f, 0.f, 0.f, 0.f};\n    float4 acc2 = {0.f, 0.f, 0.f, 0.f};\n    float4 acc3 = {0.f, 0.f, 0.f, 0.f};\n    \n    size_t i = tid;\n    for (; i + 3*stride < n; i += stride * 4) {\n        float4 v0 = buf[i];\n        float4 v1 = buf[i + stride];\n        float4 v2 = buf[i + 2*stride];\n        float4 v3 = buf[i + 3*stride];\n        acc0.x += v0.x; acc0.y += v0.y; acc0.z += v0.z; acc0.w += v0.w;\n        acc1.x += v1.x; acc1.y += v1.y; acc1.z += v1.z; acc1.w += v1.w;\n        acc2.x += v2.x; acc2.y += v2.y; acc2.z += v2.z; acc2.w += v2.w;\n        acc3.x += v3.x; acc3.y += v3.y; acc3.z += v3.z; acc3.w += v3.w;\n    }\n    // Handle remainder\n    for (; i < n; i += stride) {\n        float4 v = buf[i];\n        acc0.x += v.x; acc0.y += v.y; acc0.z += v.z; acc0.w += v.w;\n    }\n    \n    // Combine accumulators\n    acc0.x += acc1.x + acc2.x + acc3.x;\n    acc0.y += acc1.y + acc2.y + acc3.y;\n    acc0.z += acc1.z + acc2.z + acc3.z;\n    acc0.w += acc1.w + acc2.w + acc3.w;\n    float mysum = acc0.x + acc0.y + acc0.z + acc0.w;\n    \n    // Block reduction\n    __shared__ float sdata[256];\n    sdata[threadIdx.x] = mysum;\n    __syncthreads();\n    for (int s = blockDim.x / 2; s > 0; s >>= 1) {\n        if (threadIdx.x < s) sdata[threadIdx.x] += sdata[threadIdx.x + s];\n        __syncthreads();\n    }\n    if (threadIdx.x == 0) sink[blockIdx.x] = sdata[0];\n}\n```\n\nWait, the strided access pattern with stride = grid*block might not be the best for coalescing. Let me think.\n\nFor coalescing, consecutive threads in a wavefront should read consecutive memory addresses. With my pattern:\n- thread 0 reads buf[0], buf[stride], buf[2*stride], ...\n- thread 1 reads buf[1], buf[stride+1], ...\n\nSo in iteration 0, threads 0..63 read buf[0..63] — that's consecutive float4s = consecutive 16-byte chunks. A wavefront of 64 threads reads 64*16 = 1024 bytes, which is 8 cache lines (128 bytes each). This is perfectly coalesced. Good.\n\nBut with the unroll-by-4, in the unrolled body:\n- v0: threads read buf[0..63]\n- v1: threads read buf[stride..stride+63]\n- v2: threads read buf[2*stride..2*stride+63]\n- v3: threads read buf[3*stride..3*stride+63]\n\nEach of these is coalesced. The four loads can be in flight simultaneously, exposing ILP. Good.\n\nNow, the stride is grid*block*4 (with unroll). With grid=2048, block=256, stride = 2048*256*4 = 2097152 float4s = 8 MiB. So each thread's reads are 8 MiB apart. The 4 in-flight loads are at offsets 0, 8MiB, 16MiB, 24MiB. These are in different L2 cache sets, which is good for avoiding cache thrashing... actually for HBM bandwidth we don't care about L2.\n\nHmm wait, actually for a streaming read benchmark, the access pattern doesn't matter much as long as it's coalesced. The L2/L3 will be bypassed naturally because the working set (4 GiB) is way bigger than L3 (224 MiB).\n\nActually wait — MI35X has 224 MiB of L3 (Infinity Cache). That's significant! 4 GiB is ~18x the L3, so each pass will evict everything. But across iterations, the L3 will be warm with the last 224 MiB of the previous pass. That's only 5% of the buffer, so the second iteration gets ~95% HBM hits. Good enough.\n\nActually, to be really safe and bust the cache, I could make the buffer even larger, like 8 GiB. That's way more than enough.\n\nBut wait — too many blocks might cause register spilling or reduced occupancy. Wait, no — hipEventRecord records timestamps, and hipEventElapsedTime gives the difference between two events. If I record event_before[i] and event_after[i] around dispatch i, then hipEventElapsedTime(&t, event_before[i], event_after[i]) gives the duration of dispatch i. This works even if dispatches are pipelined, because the events are recorded on the GPU's timeline.\n\nBut wait, if dispatches are pipelined (no sync between them), then event_after[i] and event_before[i+1] are very close (back-to-back). So event_before[0], dispatch 0, event_after[0], event_before[1], dispatch 1, event_after[1], ... on the GPU timeline. hipEventElapsedTime(event_before[i], event_after[i]) = duration of dispatch i. \n\nLet me write the host code:\n\n```cpp\nint main() {\n    // Set device 0\n    hipSetDevice(0);\n    \n    const size_t buffer_bytes = (size_t)4 * 1024 * 1024 * 1024;  // 4 GiB\n    const size_t n_float4 = buffer_bytes / sizeof(float4);\n    \n    // Allocate buffer\n    float4* d_buf;\n    hipMalloc(&d_buf, buffer_bytes);\n    \n    // Initialize buffer (to avoid any NaN issues and warm up)\n    hipMemset(d_buf, 0, buffer_bytes);\n    \n    // Allocate sink\n    const int grid_size = 2048;\n    float* d_sink;\n    hipMalloc(&d_sink, grid_size * sizeof(float));\n    \n    // Warmup\n    for (int i = 0; i < n_iters; i++) {\n        hipEventElapsedTime(&times[i], starts[i], stops[i]);\n    }\n    \n    std::sort(times.begin(), times.end());\n    float median_ms = times[n_iters / 2];\n    \n    double bandwidth_gbs = (double)buffer_bytes / (median_ms * 1e-3) / 1e9;\n    \n    printf(\"HBM_BANDWIDTH_GBPS=%.2f\\n\", bandwidth_gbs);\n    printf(\"buffer_bytes=%zu\\n\", buffer_bytes);\n    \n    // Cleanup\n    hipFree(d_buf);\n    hipFree(d_sink);\n    for (int i = 0; Instead of manual unrolling with multiple accumulators, let me use `#pragma unroll` on the inner loop. The compiler will unroll and pipeline. This is simpler and the compiler is good at this.\n\n```cpp\nfor (size_t i = tid; i < n; i += stride) {\n    float4 v = buf[i];\n    acc.x += v.x; acc.y += v.y; acc.z += v.z; acc.w += v.w;\n}\n```\n\nWith `#pragma unroll 4`, the compiler unrolls by 4 and issues 4 loads that can be in flight. This should work well.\n\nBut actually, with a single accumulator, the compiler might serialize the additions (each add depends on the previous). The loads will be pipelined regardless of the add dependency chain.\n\nHmm, but to be safe, let me use multiple accumulators. It's a well-known technique for bandwidth benchmarks. Let me write it manually.\n\nOK here's my final kernel design:\n\n```cpp\n__global__ void __launch_bounds__(256) read_bandwidth_kernel(\n    const float4* __restrict__ buf,\n    size_t n,\n    float* __restrict__ sink) {\n    \n    const size_t tid = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x;\n    const size_t total_threads = (size_t)gridDim.x * blockDim.x;\n    const size_t stride = total_threads * 4;  // unroll by 4\n    \n    float4 a0 = {0,0,0,0}, a1 = {0,0,0,0}, a2 = {0,0,0,0}, a3 = {0,0,0,0};\n    \n    size_t i = tid;\n    for (; i + 3 * total_threads < n; i += stride) {\n        float4 v0 = buf[i];\n        float4 v1 = buf[i + total_threads];\n        float4 v2 = buf[i + 2 * total_threads];\n        float4 v3 = buf[i + 3 * total_threads];\n        a0.x += v0.x; a0.y += v0.y; a0.z += v0.z; a0.w += v0.w;\n        a1.x += v1.x; a1.y += v1.y; a1.z += v1.z; a1.w += v1.w;\n        a2.x += v2.x; a2.y += v2.y; a2.z += v2.z; a2.w += v2.w;\n        a3.x += v3.x; a3.y += v3.y; a3.z += v3.z; a3.w += v3.w;\n    }\n    // Remainder\n    for (; i < n; i += total_threads) {\n        float4 v = buf[i];\n        a0.x += v.x; a0.y += v.y; a0.z += v.z; a0.w += v.w;\n    }\n    \n    // Combine\n    a0.x += a1.x + a2.x + a3.x;\n    a0.y += a1.y + a2.y + a3.y;\n    a0.z += a1.z + a2.z + a3.z;\n    a0.w += a1.w + a2.w + a3.w;\n    float sum = a0.x + a0.y + a0.z + a0.w;\n    \n    // Block reduction\n    __shared__ float sdata[256];\n    sdata[threadIdx.x] = sum;\n    __syncthreads();\n    for (int s = 128; s > 0; s >>= 1) {\n        if (threadIdx.x < s) sdata[threadIdx.x] += sdata[threadIdx.x + s];\n        __syncthreads();\n    }\n    if (threadIdx.x == 0) {\n        sink[blockIdx.x] = sdata[0];\n    }\n}\n```\n\nWait, there's an issue with the unrolled loop. Consecutive threads (tid, tid+1) read consecutive elements. So within a wavefront, reads are coalesced. Good.\n\nThe 4 in-flight loads are at tid, tid+TT, tid+2*TT, tid+3*TT. These are far apart in memory (TT = 524288 float4s = 8 MiB). So they access different memory pages/banks. Good for parallelism.\n\nLet me make sure the loop bound is correct:\n- `i + 3 * total_threads < n` ensures all 4 reads are in bounds\n- After the unrolled loop, `i` might be < n, so the remainder loop handles the rest\n\nThis looks correct.\n\nNow let me think about the total_threads. i < n_total; i++) {\n        CHECK_HIP(hipEventCreateWithFlags(&starts[i], hipEventDefault));\n        CHECK_HIP(hipEventCreateWithFlags(&stops[i],  hipEventDefault));\n    }\n\n    // All dispatches use the same kernel;
